    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer17143231709368559254">"I, Hasan the son of Muhammad the weigh-master, I, Jean-Leon de Medici, circumcised at the hand of a barber and baptized at the hand of a pope, I am now called the African, but I am not from Africa, nor from Europe, nor from Arabia.   <span id="freeText17143231709368559254" style="display:none">"I, Hasan the son of Muhammad the weigh-master, I, Jean-Leon de Medici, circumcised at the hand of a barber and baptized at the hand of a pope, I am now called the African, but I am not from Africa, nor from Europe, nor from Arabia. I am also called the Granadan, the Fassi, the Zayyati, but I come from no country, from no city, no tribe. I am the son of the road, my country is the caravan, my life the most unexpected of voyages." Thus wrote Leo Africanus, in his fortieth year, in this imaginary autobiography of the famous geographer, adventurer, and scholar Hasan al-Wazzan, who was born in Granada in 1488. His family fled the Inquisition and took him to the city of Fez, in North Africa. Hasan became an itinerant merchant, and made many journeys to the East, journeys rich in adventure and observation. He was captured by a Sicilian pirate and taken back to Rome as a gift to Pope Leo X, who baptized him Johannes Leo. While in Rome, he wrote the first trilingual dictionary (Latin, Arabic and Hebrew), as well as his celebrated Description of Africa, for which he is still remembered as Leo Africanus.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17143231709368559254" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

<span id="freeTextContainer232466881402520131">Check this out...<br/><br/>'Wherever you are , some will want to ask questions about your skin or your prayers. Beware of gratifying their instincts, my son, beware of bending before the multitude! Muslim, Jew or Christian, they must take you as you are, or lose you. When men's minds seem narrow to you, tell yourself that the land of God is broad; broad His hands and broad His heart. Never hesitate to go far away, beyond all seas, all frontiers, all countries, all beliefs.' (p.360)<br/><br/>Now go and read it!!!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_80964029" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/80964029" />
    <a title="Kelly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/94602-kelly"><img alt="Kelly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1521304176p2/94602.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/80964029?book_show_action=true">Dec 14, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kelly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kelly" href="/user/show/94602-kelly">Kelly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Kelly by: </span>
      Gary
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=beyond-the-horizon">beyond-the-horizon</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=identity-crisis">identity-crisis</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=20th-century-postwar-to-late">20th-century-postwar-to-late</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=cultural-meetings">cultural-meetings</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=inshallah">inshallah</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=maghreb">maghreb</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer80964029" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6083182463881943660"><i>Leo Africanus</i> belongs to several very old traditions of storytelling. It is epic, heroic, and, joins a long line of contemporary political commentaries hidden beneath a thin veil of time and space. It is a tale based on the life of (what we’re almost sure is) a real historical figure. Leo Africanus- or al-Hassan ibn Muhammad al-Wazan as his original name likely was, was a Muslim born in Granada about the time of the Reconquista. His story begins with the voices of others, speaking to him, tellin</span>
  <span id="freeText6083182463881943660" style="display:none"><i>Leo Africanus</i> belongs to several very old traditions of storytelling. It is epic, heroic, and, joins a long line of contemporary political commentaries hidden beneath a thin veil of time and space. It is a tale based on the life of (what we’re almost sure is) a real historical figure. Leo Africanus- or al-Hassan ibn Muhammad al-Wazan as his original name likely was, was a Muslim born in Granada about the time of the Reconquista. His story begins with the voices of others, speaking to him, telling him who he is- we follow his family into the large exile community of Granadans in Fez, Morroco, and then, slowly, follow Hasan himself as his story graduaally starts to become his own. We’re given a rich, wonderfully filled out picture of the Mediterranean and North Africa at the turn of the 16th century- from Al-Andalus to the Maghrib, from Timbuktu to the Sahara, downtown Cairo to wild independent mountain territories of Africa, Barbary pirate controlled port towns to Ottoman Constantinople, to Italy in the prime of the Renaissance. This is the great strength of the book. The reader is given a much more rounded picture of this era than is often typical in the West- one that does not care what the hell Christopher thinks he can find in the West Indies, or the martial squabbles of Henry of England. Maalouf is excellent at showing us a world balanced and mixed between East and West, where it was a very real possibility that a Sultan might rule in Rome, or the Castilians might decide to create an empire in North Africa. Each little place visited has it’s own proud history that matters very much- and who are you over there to think that yours matters more? He does a wonderful job at showing us how absolutely meaningless any kind of border we create is, whether physical or mental. What’s great about Leo(/Hasan/whoever he is at the moment) is that he seems both the essence of his time, in the thick of each development, and yet an escapee from history, able to look like many different people, have many different names (as with all exiles' lit, this book is all about the names) while remaining himself despite it all. Maalouf’s addition to the beatitudes is: <i>”Blessed are the outsiders,”</i> a blessing that his main character is both tortured and exonerated by. It is hard not to be moved by Maalouf’s movingly expressed ultimate mission of tolerance and peace- especially when we know that the 40 years of bloody, pointless, absurd conflict that Leo Africanus witnesses is a stand in for an ongoing contemporary conflict that has lasted more than 40 years now and shows no signs of stopping. Despite being published in 1986, this book remains, sadly, as relevant today as the day it was published.<br /><br />However. And it's a very unfortunate however- I do have to detract some points for the technical construction of this novel. It is an epic, as I said, and since it is trying to give us a history of 40 years of this area of the world, our main character is required to be in a lot of places and meet a lot of people. We're never in one place for very long, which for one thing makes the story rather disjointed and underdeveloped, and for another... a real person would need a miracle to make it happen. And so he gets miracles. Lots of them. His progress is frankly ridiculously unbelievable. It requires every other person he meets to “take a special liking to him,” and give him amazing gifts, money, opportunities that allow him to progress to the next unbelievable meeting- he sees everyone from Raphael of Urbino and Pope Leo to the Ottoman Sultan, the pirate Barbarossa, and kings from lands near and far. Unfortunately, our character is not at all developed- one assumes due to time constraints in getting him all over Africa and Europe- so we have no idea why he’s so special, and nor are we given much of a reason to care. We are supposed to like him, as he voices a number of modern approved political opinions, but the dispersal of these feels cheap. And also, I just have to note, his character does a number of despicable things that are hard to forgive- such as repeatedly abandoning his wives and children, and even, on one occasion, having sex with his wife on their wedding night despite the fact that she faints away that the prospect (she's quite sheltered and religious). When he’s taken to task for this by one of his wives, we get some answer about how “you just can’t tie me down, baby!,” that’s supposed to tie into his exile, rootless nature, but really just reads as a cheap excuse for him to get on with the next scene of his life, since Maalouf has said all he wants to say involving the particular storyline that woman is a part of. <br /><br />There are one or two developed characters- Hasan/Leo’s best friend Hurun, for example, one of his wives, and at one point his father (who serves as more of a representative sample, but it still works), but these are picked up and put down as our Hero needs them to continue on. I love exile lit, don’t get me wrong, but it is at it’s most powerful where we believe that the main character is exiled <i>from something</i>- this particular character doesn’t seem to have much of a stake in anything- he’s able to abandon each thing as necessary and we rarely see him carry over any wounds from one “book” of his story to another. It doesn’t help that the story is told in a very impersonal tone, a tone that struck me as fairly unbelievable most of the time- after all, the story is meant to be him writing down his life for his young son- and only in the “and here’s the message, kids,” interludes between the volumes of his story do we see any of that- like the author himself sometimes forgets his format. I didn’t feel at all emotionally attached to this book, and I really wanted to. It’s just such a shame.<br /><br />This book should’ve either been much longer, or found a way to give us it’s messages without the “Where’s Waldo?” round Maalouf he had to write before he could make the kind of universal statements he wanted to make here. In the end, I felt like I was being called to witness something when what I really wanted (and what he gave us in those all too brief glimpses) was to get to know someone who had witnessed, and survived.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6083182463881943660" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=read-2014">read-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=africa">africa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer421189739" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9445279743840108726">Leo Africanus is a wonderfully realized, imaginary autobiography of one Hasan of Granada, driven from Spain along with his family at the time of the Inquisition. From there his life was to follow the edges of the Mediterranean in a tale that covers the years from his birth in 1488 through the end of his writing in 1527. He lands in Fez with his family, travels to Timbuktu, Tunis, Constaninople and Cairo. He is involved in education, in trade, suffers horribly and also has wonderful gains---perso</span>
  <span id="freeText9445279743840108726" style="display:none">Leo Africanus is a wonderfully realized, imaginary autobiography of one Hasan of Granada, driven from Spain along with his family at the time of the Inquisition. From there his life was to follow the edges of the Mediterranean in a tale that covers the years from his birth in 1488 through the end of his writing in 1527. He lands in Fez with his family, travels to Timbuktu, Tunis, Constaninople and Cairo. He is involved in education, in trade, suffers horribly and also has wonderful gains---personally and professionally.<br /><br />And then he is taken by pirates and shipped to Rome where he becomes a gift of the Pope, Pope Leo X. Thus he becomes Leo Africanus of the title.<br /><br />And what a story Leo tells. The history of the Mediterranean cultures of his time, it is all there to be seen through his eyes and experience. Maalouf makes you feel that Hasan/Leo lived, breathed as you or I. There is one passage in particular that I want to add to the review:<br /><br /><br /> <i>Wherever you are, some will want to ask questions<br /> about your skin or your prayers. Beware of gratifying<br /> their instincts, my son, beware of bending before the<br /> multitude! Muslim, Jew or Christian, they must take you<br /> as you are, or lose you. When men's minds seem narrow<br /> to you, tell yourself that the land of God is broad; <br /> broad His hands and broad His heart. Never hesitate to<br /> go far away, beyond all seas, all frontiers, all countries,<br /> all beliefs.</i> (p 360)<br /><br /><br /><br />I will keep this book on my shelf along side my copy of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/142395.Samarkand" title="Samarkand by Amin Maalouf" rel="nofollow noopener">Samarkand</a> and hopefully read them both again. But first I will seek out other works by the author.<br /><br />Highly recommended 4.5</span>
  <a data-text-id="9445279743840108726" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3040526286" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3040526286" />
    <a title="Kavita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5858699-kavita"><img alt="Kavita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1315387023p2/5858699.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3040526286?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kavita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kavita" href="/user/show/5858699-kavita">Kavita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5858699-kavita?shelf=egypt">egypt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5858699-kavita?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5858699-kavita?shelf=italy">italy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5858699-kavita?shelf=morocco">morocco</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5858699-kavita?shelf=spain">spain</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3040526286" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4262658596244285180">Joannes Leo Africanus alias al-Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Wazzan al-Fasi (whew, that name is longer than mine!) was a 16th century traveller, diplomat, and geographer, whose works became quickly famous and for a long time, were a source of information in Europe about the Islamic kingdoms to the East and Africa. Both European and African, Christian and Muslim, Leo was a fascinating man with a fascinating life. He's a subject not many have tackled before, so full kudos to Amin Maalouf for taking it on.</span>
  <span id="freeText4262658596244285180" style="display:none">Joannes Leo Africanus alias al-Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Wazzan al-Fasi (whew, that name is longer than mine!) was a 16th century traveller, diplomat, and geographer, whose works became quickly famous and for a long time, were a source of information in Europe about the Islamic kingdoms to the East and Africa. Both European and African, Christian and Muslim, Leo was a fascinating man with a fascinating life. He's a subject not many have tackled before, so full kudos to Amin Maalouf for taking it on. <br /><br /><i>Leo Africanus</i> is written in four parts: Granada, Fez, Cairo, and Rome, the four homes of Leo. From his birth till the Sack of Rome, covering a period of around 40 years, Maalouf follows Leo from city to city, country to country, continent to continent. It's a history-rich narrative that really focuses more on the tumultuous events of the 16th century than on Leo. It's evocatively done, and you get a very good sense of not just how these events panned out, but also daily life in Muslim and Christian lands of the time. <br /><br />Maalouf's research is extensive and he has a pretty good understanding of his historical subjects. But as not much is known about Leo, beyond what he himself had written in his works, the author had to fill in the blanks. He indulges in literary licence to a large extent while doing this. The plot regarding his sister's ordeals appear to be all made up, and serve more as a way to express how certain things operated than to take forward Leo's story. I haven't been able to find out that Harun Pasha existed at all. There were many such instances in this book, and they were designed to give a more rounded understanding of life then.<br /><br />So despite all the amazing things in this book, it lacks one major component: emotion. Leo keeps travelling on and on, and his relationships with places appear more evocative than those with people around him. The man runs through multiple wives: moving from one to the other with remarkable ease. Though the book is written as a note to his son, Leo simply doesn't appear to have much of a relationship with his children either. The part covering his childhood was better in this respect, but as he grew up, these relationships faded away from the picture.<br /><br />I think this might have worked so much better in a different format, similar to Elena Ferrante's <i>Neapolitan Novels</i>. With multiple books, Leo might actually have got some character development, and the missing pieces suitably filled. Nevertheless, this is a very good book and I would highly recommend it to anyone. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4262658596244285180" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_22067485" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/22067485" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/90686-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1179691570p2/90686.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/22067485?book_show_action=true">May 12, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/90686-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer22067485" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1917936392169052417">Although the premise of this book (based on the travels of the actual Leo Africanus) was what drew me to it, it really suffered from a disjointed plot. Leo's travels are what makes him unique: he is one of the few historical figures who demonstrated the blend of Muslim, Jewish, and Christian faiths around the fall of Granada. Yet the reader gets almost no sense of the society in which he fits. His numerous travels seem to have no connection to one another and the characters around him suffer fro</span>
  <span id="freeText1917936392169052417" style="display:none">Although the premise of this book (based on the travels of the actual Leo Africanus) was what drew me to it, it really suffered from a disjointed plot. Leo's travels are what makes him unique: he is one of the few historical figures who demonstrated the blend of Muslim, Jewish, and Christian faiths around the fall of Granada. Yet the reader gets almost no sense of the society in which he fits. His numerous travels seem to have no connection to one another and the characters around him suffer from a serious case of being two-dimensional. I kept reading to try and see what I was missing, figuring it was me who just wasn't getting into the story enough or not paying enough attention. Well, having finished it, I realized that it wasn't my fault as a reader, there simply just wasn't enough of a plot to hold the book together. Which is a crime, as the travels of this man could certainly have filled more than the scant 300 pages of the book. Leo Africanus' life is one of the most perfect out of history to be turned into historical fiction and it's upsetting that it was botched so badly. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1917936392169052417" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_444367446" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/444367446" />
    <a title="Ms.pegasus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5015851-ms-pegasus"><img alt="Ms.pegasus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1335705124p2/5015851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/444367446?book_show_action=true">Apr 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ms.pegasus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ms.pegasus" href="/user/show/5015851-ms-pegasus">Ms.pegasus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19289413-leo-africanus">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone interested in history</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Ms.pegasus by: </span>
      Christopher Lydon's "Kickstart" podcast
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5015851-ms-pegasus?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5015851-ms-pegasus?shelf=translated-into-english">translated-into-english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5015851-ms-pegasus?shelf=history">history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer444367446" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14811012408540157289">Reading Amin Maalouf's book is a bit like viewing McArthur's Universal Corrective Map of the World. In that map, north is at the bottom and south at the top. In addition Asia and the Pacific Ocean are in the center. It's a jarring inversion of perspective. In the world history class I took in college, the key figures were Ferdinand and Isabella, Charles V, and Martin Luther. The overarching theme was the rise of the nation state. Maalouf's narrative is through Muslim eyes. His protagonist is a c</span>
  <span id="freeText14811012408540157289" style="display:none">Reading Amin Maalouf's book is a bit like viewing McArthur's Universal Corrective Map of the World. In that map, north is at the bottom and south at the top. In addition Asia and the Pacific Ocean are in the center. It's a jarring inversion of perspective. In the world history class I took in college, the key figures were Ferdinand and Isabella, Charles V, and Martin Luther. The overarching theme was the rise of the nation state. Maalouf's narrative is through Muslim eyes. His protagonist is a cosmopolitan polyglot, a self-described citizen of nowhere and everywhere, born in 1494 as Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Wazzan. In his world political boundaries blur and change with alarming rapidity. In his world the salient entities are enclaves of displaced refugees — Jews, Conversos, Granadans, Circassians, Berbers, Arabs, and Genoans — and the trade routes connecting Mediterranean ports and cities threading the passes of the Atlas Mountains, the oases of the Sahara, and the centers of the Songhai Empire in west Africa (covering present day Mali and Ghana). <br /><br />Maalouf offers no maps in this book. Perhaps that is intentional. In this world locales like Sijilmassa, Ouarzazate (door of the desert), Tabalbala, and the Taghaza salt mines are as familiar as Timbuktu, Fez, Cairo and Rome. A map would only serve to reinforce our preconceptions about the importance and continuity of these places when the narrative is a succession of accelerating discontinuities. Actual polities had blurred boundaries determined by the fortunes of warfare and the purses of warlords, constantly struggling to pay their armies. This was the world of Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Wazzan, Hasan the Granadan, baptized by Pope Leo X as Jean Leon de Medici, and now known by his pen name Leo Africanus.<br /><br />The book is written in the guise of a memoir addressed to Guiseppe (born in Rome) — or Yusuf if the Arabic version is preferred, Hasan's son. It is divided into four sections: The Book of Granada (1489-1494), The Book of Fez (1495-1513), The Book of Cairo (1513-1519), and the Book of Rome (1519-1527). Within each section, the chapters are labeled by year according to the Muslim lunar calendar. Despite this straight-forward chronological progression, the historical events are disorienting. Few western readers will be familiar with the key actors in Hasan's life. How many of you are familiar with Boabdil (Muhammad XII, exiled Sultan of Granada), Muhammad the Portuguese (Mohammed al-Burtuqali, Sultan of Fez), Al-Ashraf Tumanbay (Tumanbay II, the last Sultan of Cairo), or Salim the Grim (Salim I, Sultan of the Ottoman Empire)? Suleiman the Magnificent may strike a more familiar chord, but possibly only in the context of his defeat at the gates of Vienna in 1529. Yet, each of these men had a distinct personality and political agenda that affected the course of history.<br /><br />My cursory reading of the first two sections caused me some initial confusion. Many of the events pre-date Hasan's birth; others occur during his early childhood. He tells the story through the vantage point of his mother Salma, his father Muhammed, and his maternal uncle Khali, and the first person point of view is not spoken in his own voice. His voice assumes the narration as he reaches adolescence. He develops friendships with two unlikely boys as a student, the clever Harun the Ferret, and the enigmatic Ahmad the Lame One. He encounters and represses his first sexual stirrings with his half-sister Mariam. He is estranged from his father and only develops sympathy for his predicament far into his adult years. <br /><br />These domestic travails are folded into Hasan's observations in a caravan where he accompanies his maternal uncle Khali. However, Maalouf avoids the exoticism of a travelogue, although he does relate some fantastical stories gleaned from some of the people they encounter. Instead, Maalouf's novel derives much of its flavor from Hasan's shrewd observations. He describes Ferdinand's advance after taking possession of the Alhambra. <i> “A major war unfolded, which the Muslims could not win, but which, if they could not have avoided, they could at least have delayed. It was to last ten years and end in the most ignominious manner possible. In addition, it was accompanied by a bloody and demoralizing civil war, so often the fate of kingdoms on their way to extinction.”</i> (p.18) He reflects on the intellectual decline precipitated by civil war. <i> “And then came the drying up of the Spirit and of the pen. To defend themselves against the ideas and customs of the Franks, men turned Tradition into a citadel in which they shut themselves up. Granada could only produce imitators without talent or boldness.”</i> (p.37) <br /><br />Maalouf displays his gift for storytelling through the voices of his characters. Hasan's father Muhammed describes the unease during Boabdil's reign: <i> “On this autumn day, the yellowing leaves were more securely attached to the trees than the notables of Granada to their monarch. The city was divided as it had been for years, between the peace party and the war party, neither of which called upon the sultan.”</i> (p.24)<br /><br />Maalouf imbues Hasan with a quiet but steady moral compass. Despite his political instincts which favor an Ottoman victory that would free Granada from Spanish dominion, he warns his hosts in Cairo of an impending Ottoman attack in order to secure the lives of his Circassian wife and her son Bayezid, a descendant of the Ottoman royal lineage.<br /><br />In Rome he is able to reconcile his Islamic beliefs with those of his Christian hosts, finding common though uncomfortable ground with even his student Hans, an enthusiastic proponent of Martin Luther. Later, Pope Clement VII probes his religious beliefs: <i> “'Would not religion have been the best of all ways of life for a man of learning and education like yourself?'”</i> Hasan responds: <i> “'To speak of religion in the Holy Father's presence is like speaking of one's fiancée in her father's presence.'”</i> Pressed, he continues: <i> “'If the head of the Church was not listening to me, I would say that religion teaches men humility, but that it has none itself. I would say that all religions have produced both saints and murderers, with an equally good conscience. That in the life of this city, there are the Clement years and the Adrian [the preceding pope, an intolerant zealot and former inquisitor] years, between which religion does not allow you to choose....Muslims learn that the best of men is the most useful to mankind but in spite of such words, they sometimes honour false zealots more than real benefactors.'”</i> (p.329) For Maalouf, humility is the foundation of religion.<br /><br />This was not an easy book to read. I spent a great deal of time looking up the historical characters and events in order to gain some context. His prose is more contemplative than dramatic. Even an episode about his new bride Fatima elicits more intellectual mirth than outright laughter. Nevertheless, this is an important book written by an author who has thought deeply about the way we view history and our responsibility to a humanistic outlook.<br /><br />NOTES:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.flourish.org/upsidedownmap">https://www.flourish.org/upsidedownmap</a> is an interesting examination of the perspective of an upside down map.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://pen-international.org/news/in-conversation-with-the-renowned-novelist-amin-maalouf">http://pen-international.org/news/in-...</a> an interview with the author, conducted in 2014<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://authorscalendar.info/maalouf.htm">http://authorscalendar.info/maalouf.htm</a> provides a succinct profile of the author</span>
  <a data-text-id="14811012408540157289" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_622978427" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/622978427" />
    <a title="Gary Hoggatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8844957-gary-hoggatt"><img alt="Gary Hoggatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1453675555p2/8844957.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/622978427?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gary Hoggatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gary Hoggatt" href="/user/show/8844957-gary-hoggatt">Gary Hoggatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8844957-gary-hoggatt?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8844957-gary-hoggatt?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8844957-gary-hoggatt?shelf=all-fiction">all-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer622978427" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3063280825053976208">One might not expect a geographer to be a great inspiration for historical fiction. But when that geographer travels the lands he chronicles at a time of great upheaval and turmoil and becomes embroiled in every sort of adventure and meets major historic figures along the way, it can indeed become a lively and engaging novel. Such is the case with the titular Moorish traveler in Amin Maalouf's 1986 book, Leo Africanus.<br/><br/>Born as al-Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Wazzan al-Fasi in Granada in the 1490's, Hasa</span>
  <span id="freeText3063280825053976208" style="display:none">One might not expect a geographer to be a great inspiration for historical fiction. But when that geographer travels the lands he chronicles at a time of great upheaval and turmoil and becomes embroiled in every sort of adventure and meets major historic figures along the way, it can indeed become a lively and engaging novel. Such is the case with the titular Moorish traveler in Amin Maalouf's 1986 book, Leo Africanus.<br /><br />Born as al-Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Wazzan al-Fasi in Granada in the 1490's, Hasan is forced into exile as a child after the Spanish Reconquista. This sets the tone for his life, as he's constantly on the move. He and his family move to Fez, Morocco, with other refugees, where Hasan comes of age and begins one of his many runs of success. On the way back to Fez from Timbuktu on a diplomatic mission, he happens to be in Cairo when the Ottoman Empire conquers Egypt. Later, he's abducted by Spanish pirates, taken to Rome, and sold to the Pope, who befriends him, baptizes him as Leo Africanus, and employs him as a diplomat to the Ottoman's. Oh, and at the same time as Leo is in Rome, Martin Luther is starting the Reformation. While in Italy, Leo begins the geography of Africa and an Arabic dictionary for which he is remembered.<br /><br />That brief summary is just the tip of the iceberg. Maalouf does a fantastic job of placing you in the varied and exotic places Leo finds himself and really puts you right there with Leo through all his highs and lows. As exciting as the major political action is, Maalouf vividly brings to life Leo's personal journey as well. You really begin to understand the way this eternal nomad feels as the tide of fate carries him on, and the troubles and joys he finds along the way, in his family, romantic, and professional lives.<br /><br />Leo Africanus really is an excellent example of historical fiction at its finest. Not only do you get to see interesting historical events up close or understand how ancient societies worked, but you get an intensely personal and touching story as well. Maalouf writes the novel in the form of a memoir from Africanus to his son, and there's a wonderful sense of introspection in the work.<br /><br />Maalouf also writes beautifully. The language, the pacing, the descriptions - the novel at times almost feels like poetry, but it never veers into self-indulgence. It's even more amazing when considering that the novel was originally published in French and translated by historian Peter Sluglett, who is to be commended for rendering the work in English so well.<br /><br />I highly recommend Leo Africanus. It does everything historical fiction aims for and does so in a beautiful, poetic, thoughtful voice. I certainly plan to read more from Maalouf, both historical fiction and history. He's a fantastic writer and is very talented at bringing you right into the scene, the time, the society he's describing. Alright, enough already - go get yourself a copy Leo Africanus and get started!</span>
  <a data-text-id="3063280825053976208" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_778561329" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/778561329" />
    <a title="Matthew Hunter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3040804-matthew-hunter"><img alt="Matthew Hunter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495642903p2/3040804.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/778561329?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew Hunter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew Hunter" href="/user/show/3040804-matthew-hunter">Matthew Hunter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3040804-matthew-hunter?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3040804-matthew-hunter?shelf=lebanese-literature">lebanese-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer778561329" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8548917960163218794">Good God <i>Leo Africanus</i> is a great book! It's epic in scope, reminding me a bit of <i>Forrest Gump</i> in the way that Hasan/Leo keeps popping up during major historical events. His family runs into Christopher Columbus before the explorer heads to the New World. He's present in Cairo as the armies of the Grand Turk overwhelm the city. He's holed up in Castel San Angelo as hordes friendly to Emperor Charles sack the "Eternal City". He watches from a hilltop the complete destruction of Timbuktu by fire. </span>
  <span id="freeText8548917960163218794" style="display:none">Good God <i>Leo Africanus</i> is a great book! It's epic in scope, reminding me a bit of <i>Forrest Gump</i> in the way that Hasan/Leo keeps popping up during major historical events. His family runs into Christopher Columbus before the explorer heads to the New World. He's present in Cairo as the armies of the Grand Turk overwhelm the city. He's holed up in Castel San Angelo as hordes friendly to Emperor Charles sack the "Eternal City". He watches from a hilltop the complete destruction of Timbuktu by fire. His family flees Granada after it falls to the Castilian Christians of Ferdinand and Isabella - aka Columbus's sugar daddies.<br /><br />Imagine being held in high regard by Popes, emperors, and Shaikhs, one day playing diplomat for Sultans and hearing the Muezzin's call to prayer, the next day being baptized by Pope Leo X, hanging out with the Medicis, and having Rafael ask to paint you. That's the ecclectic life of our Hasan/Leo.<br /><br />I'm biased toward books that give me the opportunity to think about important matters in creative ways. Maalouf didn't disalpoint me. Here's one example out of many:<blockquote><br />"If death was not inevitable, man would have wasted his whole life attempting to avoid it. He would have risked nothing, attempted nothing, undertaken nothing, invented nothing, built nothing. Life would have been a perpetual convalescence. Yes my brothers, let us thank God for having made us this gift of death, so that life is to have meaning; of night, that day is to have meaning; silence, that speech is to have meaning; illness, that health is to have meaning; war, that peace is to have meaning. Let us give thanks to Him for having given us weariness and pain, so that rest and joy are to have meaning. Let us give thanks to Him, Whose wisdom is infinite."</blockquote><br />Outside of the diversity of teachings about the significance of Christ's crucifixion and resurrection, I've seldom encountered the idea of death as gift. Great! Now I have multiple reasons to stop obsessing about my own mortality! :)<br /><br />It's not only the material between the covers that makes <i>Leo Africanus</i> great. The cover includes a detail from Peeter Pauwel Rubens' <i>Mulay Ahmad</i>. It's beautiful. Check out the way Rubens depicts light reflecting in his subject's eyes, on the tip of his nose, and his lips. The guy had skill! One of my favorite covers ever.<br /><br />An easy 5 stars for me. Enjoy!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8548917960163218794" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1267333832" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1267333832" />
    <a title="Ingy Higazy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16275841-ingy-higazy"><img alt="Ingy Higazy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1388756118p2/16275841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1267333832?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ingy Higazy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ingy Higazy" href="/user/show/16275841-ingy-higazy">Ingy Higazy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1267333832" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7630186809141428885">I am spellbound. And I blame myself for the times when I used to carry this book around in my backpack last summer for days without laying hands on it. I began reading the book again -from the very first chapter- a few ago. I was naturally drawn to it (and actually purchased it) because of the first book, "The Book of Granada." Maalouf had indeed taken me on a journey through many "accursed cities," Granada and elsewhere, yes. But most importantly, it was his account of Hasan's/Leo's reflections</span>
  <span id="freeText7630186809141428885" style="display:none">I am spellbound. And I blame myself for the times when I used to carry this book around in my backpack last summer for days without laying hands on it. I began reading the book again -from the very first chapter- a few ago. I was naturally drawn to it (and actually purchased it) because of the first book, "The Book of Granada." Maalouf had indeed taken me on a journey through many "accursed cities," Granada and elsewhere, yes. But most importantly, it was his account of Hasan's/Leo's reflections on, feelings, and judgements about 'home and exile' that was particularly captivating, and that appealed to me a great deal.<br /><br />I am already writing about the book here, a few hours after laying it to rest. Yet, they are words that sound more like a frenzied text to a friend than a book review. I don't think that matters much now. Seriously though, read the book if you're a little into medieval history, but also in the mood for an eloquent reflection on the meaning of home and of exile. You will undoubtedly be led to reflect yourself on, and to question, the inevitability or futility of social/cultural assimilation in our 'contemporary' world. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7630186809141428885" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2097221186" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Onur" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70534331-onur"><img alt="Onur" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508950657p2/70534331.jpg" /></a>

<div id="review_599872715" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/599872715" />
    <a title="Bjorn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19667916-bjorn"><img alt="Bjorn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1367928200p2/19667916.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/599872715?book_show_action=true">Apr 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bjorn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bjorn" href="/user/show/19667916-bjorn">Bjorn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19667916-bjorn?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19667916-bjorn?shelf=lebanon">lebanon</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer599872715" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10279146740913233842">Leo Africanus is, in a lot of ways, a good old-fashioned picaresque; Hasan is born in Granada in 1489, and when the muslims are kicked out of al-Andaluz he winds up stateless, homeless, and needing to survive (and help his family survive) by his wits. Does he travel all over the civilised world (the Mediterranean)? Of course he does. Does he meet most of the powerful people of his age, from kings to sultans to popes? Of course he does. Does he play a small but crucial part in historical events? </span>
  <span id="freeText10279146740913233842" style="display:none">Leo Africanus is, in a lot of ways, a good old-fashioned picaresque; Hasan is born in Granada in 1489, and when the muslims are kicked out of al-Andaluz he winds up stateless, homeless, and needing to survive (and help his family survive) by his wits. Does he travel all over the civilised world (the Mediterranean)? Of course he does. Does he meet most of the powerful people of his age, from kings to sultans to popes? Of course he does. Does he play a small but crucial part in historical events? You know he does.<br /><br />At the same time, the novel shares a lot with some of my favourite 20th century examples of the genre - say, Eco's Baudolino or Bengtsson's The Long Ships, in that it's very consciously written for contemporary readers. He sneaks in discussion of current topics without ever making it too obvious, has the narrator not see the prejudices of his time while still making them stand out for the reader, while at the same time showing some things starting that still influence us today but have become so much a part of culture that we don't see them. Hasan (later Leo) lives through the end of one era and the beginning of a new one, sees the fall of Granada and the rise of the Ottoman empire, the departure of Columbus and the rise of the Habsburgs, the end of small-scale mediaeval culture and the beginning of a view of society, power and religion, that claim to want to return to something old while at the same time building something entirely new - both for bad and good. Maalouf creates a fully alive, entertaining character very much of his time, to show us things about how our own time came about. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10279146740913233842" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7224461-bryn-hammond?shelf=imagined-fiction">imagined-fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer520394814" class="readable"

<div id="review_2545218956" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2545218956" />
    <a title="Geoffrey Fox" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3024716-geoffrey-fox"><img alt="Geoffrey Fox" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442467239p2/3024716.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2545218956?book_show_action=true">Sep 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Geoffrey Fox" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Geoffrey Fox" href="/user/show/3024716-geoffrey-fox">Geoffrey Fox</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780349106007" href="/book/show/290620.Leo_the_African">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2545218956" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12728950251049698415">Amin Malouf's first novel is a wonderfully vivid reimagining of the life of Hasan al-Wazzan, better known as Leo Africanus, the 16th century chronicler whose <i>Della descrittione dell’Africa et delle cose notabili che iui sono</i> was for decades Europe's principal reference work on that continent. Writing in the voice of Hasan/Leo, imagined as writing a memoir for his son, Malouf stays very close to the events in the life of this extraordinary merchant, traveler and diplomat, as drawn from Hasan's ow</span>
  <span id="freeText12728950251049698415" style="display:none">Amin Malouf's first novel is a wonderfully vivid reimagining of the life of Hasan al-Wazzan, better known as Leo Africanus, the 16th century chronicler whose <i>Della descrittione dell’Africa et delle cose notabili che iui sono</i> was for decades Europe's principal reference work on that continent. Writing in the voice of Hasan/Leo, imagined as writing a memoir for his son, Malouf stays very close to the events in the life of this extraordinary merchant, traveler and diplomat, as drawn from Hasan's own notes in his famous opus. Where Malouf's literary imagination is best displayed, however, is in the passages where he goes beyond the documents, to enter into the imagination of this highly cultivated, multilingual, dark-skinned and thickly bearded, devoutly Muslim Berber who negotiated with bandits in Morocco, was nearly killed in the Ottoman sacking of Cairo (1517), then was taken as a slave to Rome where he became a cherished diplomat to Pope Leo X (who had him convert and baptized as "Leo" but where his unusual features gave him the sobriquet "Africanus"). After the death of Pope Leo, a Medici, and of his Dutch successor Adrian VI, and then the devastation of Rome (1527) by Teutonic Lutherans and other rebels during the reign of another Medici, Pope Clement VII, Hasan was finally able to return to Africa and revert to his original faith of Islam. Conversant and highly literate in Arabic, Latin, Hebrew, Italian and possibly Turkish, he was not only a very valuable diplomat but also a prolific writer and grammarian. Only fragments of other works besides his famous Africa book have survived. <br />Malouf has Hasan born in Granada in 1488, so that he retains some infant memories of that great Muslim city before its conquest by Fernando of Aragón in 1492. This permits a very believable and lively description of the Muslim city and the immense tragedy for Muslims and Jews of its fall to the Christian monarchs Fernando and Isabel. The real Hasan was born two years after the fall, but no matter: this is a novel, not a police report, and those first scenes of Granada are among the most memorable. Later on in the story, after the adventures with bandits and "Leo's" peculiar sojourn in Rome, Malouf's restriction of his tale to documented facts, with less attention to the protagonist's mental state, lets the book wind down in intensity. However, the diminishing drama is more than compensated by the vivid recounting, from a privileged and difrom a privileged and distinguished Arab point of view. Arab point of view, of the cultures and polítical conflicts that reshaped the Mediterranean world.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12728950251049698415" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3413877764" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3413877764" />
    <a title="MadZiddi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4571120-madziddi"><img alt="MadZiddi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570091731p2/4571120.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3413877764?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="MadZiddi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="MadZiddi" href="/user/show/4571120-madziddi">MadZiddi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3413877764" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10376824068280198333">The fact that Amin Malouf's historic fiction reads like a dream ( and I have just read the heart-rending nightmare of the fall of Granada, Rome, and Cairo through eyes of a resident), might lead one to construe that historical fiction is easy to write. However, this seems very unlikely given the fact, that apart from Qazi Abdul Sattar and the Malouf, I have not come across realistic modern historic fiction. Most authors like Naseem Hijazi pander to the revisionist and romanticized historical ins</span>
  <span id="freeText10376824068280198333" style="display:none">The fact that Amin Malouf's historic fiction reads like a dream ( and I have just read the heart-rending nightmare of the fall of Granada, Rome, and Cairo through eyes of a resident), might lead one to construe that historical fiction is easy to write. However, this seems very unlikely given the fact, that apart from Qazi Abdul Sattar and the Malouf, I have not come across realistic modern historic fiction. Most authors like Naseem Hijazi pander to the revisionist and romanticized historical instincts of the masses. Not that Malouf does not take romantic liberties, but he gives a humanistic account of the European and Afro-Arabic societies on the eve of reformation and renaissance. One of the secrets of Malouf's success as a master of modern historical fiction is that instead of writing fictionalized biographies of Kings and conquerors, he has sought to build a kaleidoscope of events around the life of Middle East thinkers and adventurers like Khayyam, Mani and in this case, Hassan Al Wazzan who left an impression not only their birthplace but rather Modern Europe. The fact that this entailed them being free thinkers ahead of their times in anachronistic societies meant they were easy targets for the dogmatic establishment of the day. Such idealistic men are easy to admire and Malouf seems to find it easy to often interweave several amorous affairs, no doubt adding masala which is usually popular with readers. However, it cannot be concluded that Malouf is a formula author. Far from it, in this particular, this novel is unique in the sense that it is of an adventurer, moving from Granada, to Fez, to Cairo, Timbuktu and Rome and finally to Constantinople and making and losing fortunes in the process. painting unique portraits of the Middle East in turmoil as Christian Iberians and Turk Ottomans strangle the Arab hold in North Africa and the Catholic church is threatened by Absolute Kingship and reformation in Europe. As Hassan finds himself Castilians inquisitors in Granada, to easy-going religion in Timbuktu to conversion to Christianity in Rome, Hasan finds himself in the centre of debate between religions and keeping humanism alive in the age of zealotry. Perhaps these debates are still relevant today as religion and modernism feel iself threatened in turn by each other in Eastern and Western societies respectively.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10376824068280198333" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2785360559" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2785360559" />
    <a title="Alison" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7701175-alison"><img alt="Alison" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1562315654p2/7701175.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2785360559?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alison" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alison" href="/user/show/7701175-alison">Alison</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19289413-leo-africanus">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7701175-alison?shelf=2019challenge">2019challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7701175-alison?shelf=biography">biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7701175-alison?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2785360559" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12058903117833656921">In this gripping adventure tale, Maalouf uses the historical Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Wazzan, as a narrative structure to explore turn-of-the-sixteenth century North Africa and Spain. Maalouf brings to life a world of grand cities, lives punctuated by the call to prayer, intriguing factions, lengthy and tedious merchant caravans, and the complex world of loving polygamous households.<br/>Al-Wazzan provides a great central figure. Too much about him is unknown to make an accurate biography, but enough is</span>
  <span id="freeText12058903117833656921" style="display:none">In this gripping adventure tale, Maalouf uses the historical Hasan ibn Muhammad al-Wazzan, as a narrative structure to explore turn-of-the-sixteenth century North Africa and Spain. Maalouf brings to life a world of grand cities, lives punctuated by the call to prayer, intriguing factions, lengthy and tedious merchant caravans, and the complex world of loving polygamous households.<br />Al-Wazzan provides a great central figure. Too much about him is unknown to make an accurate biography, but enough is known to ensure that an extraordinary story is plausible, if not always likely. Maalouf uses this to create a man in love with travel, endlessly curious, who has a knack of stumbling into some of the greatest events of his time. The novel hence explores not only the Muslim world of the time, but the feeling of retreat, as the Christian expansion moves forward. Intra-Christian and intra-Muslim divisions also abound, and Maalouf draws out the complex world of shifting alliances as city states give way to vast empires, and a way of life continues to evolve.<br />The book largely works because Hasan feels so realised. You feel the tension between his love of travel and restlessness and his deep connections to family and the women in it in particular. He exists as both an insider, connected to communities and values, and an outsider, with multiple loyalties, in all the locales he visits.<br />The book's strengths are hence worldbuilding, suspense, travelogue and good characters. I wouldn't say it is especially profound, and the tone is less smooth in translation than I suspect in the original French, but I found it fun and enlightening, and such a pleasure to spend time in this spacetime place, which gets rare treatment in the stuffed historical fiction genre.<br /><br />2019 Reading Challenge #33. A book with a zodiac sign or astrology term in title</span>
  <a data-text-id="12058903117833656921" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText16666888114055449632" style="display:none">Leo Africanus is an epic travel journey seen from the eyes of our protagonist Hasan who assumes many identities over 40 years of time period depicted in the book. The journey starts in Al Andalus before the fall of Granada and winds its way through Fez, Timbuktu, Cairo, Constantinople, Mecca, and Rome amongst other cities. <span id="freeTextContainer6003209864166032755">I am a fan of historical novels. Let that be clear. But I have read better ones than this one. I'd say the story is too much of a factual acccount. Little is revealed about how the characters think or feel, or what position they take in certain conflicts or situations. <br/>Although one cannot deny that as lives go, the life that the main character led is quite unbelievable in the least, especially since it is not an imaginary story but based on facts.<br/>Maybe I would have appreciated the story more, ha</span>
  <span id="freeText6003209864166032755" style="display:none">I am a fan of historical novels. Let that be clear. But I have read better ones than this one. I'd say the story is too much of a factual acccount. Little is revealed about how the characters think or feel, or what position they take in certain conflicts or situations. <br />Although one cannot deny that as lives go, the life that the main character led is quite unbelievable in the least, especially since it is not an imaginary story but based on facts.<br />Maybe I would have appreciated the story more, had I known more about the main character and his role in history as such, but I did not feel inclined to read up on him because I felt little or no particular sympathy for him, because so little is said about his emotional life.<br />Not one that I would recommend.</span>

<div id="review_631546855" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/631546855" />
    <a title="Raja" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2006912-raja"><img alt="Raja" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1370239205p2/2006912.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/631546855?book_show_action=true">Jun 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Raja" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Raja" href="/user/show/2006912-raja">Raja</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer631546855" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3504112977253886409">This has to be one of Maalouf's best books. What a story! Meet Hasan, the son of the Granadan weigh-master - born just before the tumultuous events of 1492, when Ferdinand and Isabella invaded the last remaining Muslim kingdoms in Iberia setting in motion a period of Castilian imperialism in Europe. 1492 was also the year Cristobal Colon wandered around Santa Fe, a newly built settlement outside the besieged city of Granada, seeking an audience with the monarch, and eventually finding his way to</span>
  <span id="freeText3504112977253886409" style="display:none">This has to be one of Maalouf's best books. What a story! Meet Hasan, the son of the Granadan weigh-master - born just before the tumultuous events of 1492, when Ferdinand and Isabella invaded the last remaining Muslim kingdoms in Iberia setting in motion a period of Castilian imperialism in Europe. 1492 was also the year Cristobal Colon wandered around Santa Fe, a newly built settlement outside the besieged city of Granada, seeking an audience with the monarch, and eventually finding his way to the Caribbean - forever changing the course of history. <br /><br />Hasan's story is one of migration, movement, sometimes voluntary, often not. But nevertheless fascinatingly recounted - Granada, Fez, Cairo, Timbuktu, Tunis, Algiers, Constantinople - a city ruled by the aptly named Ottoman Sultan Salim "the Grim," pilgrimage to Mecca, then Rome, where he was baptized as John Leo the African - these are the places that Leo traveled through, lived, sometimes loved, sometimes escaped from, and most importantly, wrote about. <br /><br />What an adventure, especially since it was based on the real life of a man known to have penned the definitive work on Africa, the "Description of Africa," which remained the only meticulous account of the places, networks, peoples, and polities of the continent available to Europeans till the 18th century.<br /><br />A breathtakingly encyclopedic account of the important events and crises of the late 15th and early 16th centuries, many of which found Leo Africanus at the center, sometimes tantalizingly close to the centres of power. He converses with Sultans, Popes, befriends the great pirate leader Barbarossa, whose letter to the Ottoman Sultan he attempts to carry, witnesses the execution of the great hero of Cairo's futile defence against the Ottoman invasion, the Mameluke Tumanbey, falls in love with a widow from the Ottoman royal family who seeks revenge for her husband's death at the hands of Salim, and dreams of placing her toddler son on the throne of Constantinople.<br /><br />Maalouf's work brings out of obscurity one of the most fascinating personalities of the era. A traveller, writer, poet, diplomat, advisor, who spoke and wrote in multiple languages including Arabic, Hebrew, Castilian, Turkish, Italian, Latin, and whose life story provides a fascinating glimpse of a world undergoing multiple tectonic shifts - from the fall of Granada to the Castilians, to that of Cairo at the hands of the Ottomans, and finally to the destruction of Rome by the Germanic allies of the Holy Roman emperor, Charles the Castilian - all witnessed and commented upon by Leo the Granadan. <br /><br />Maalouf's writing style also lends itself admirably to the piecing together of the story of Leo Africanus. There are truly beautiful passages, one of which I quote below:<br /><br /><i>"When the wayfarers know that, for weeks and months, they must proceed in the same direction, confront the same perils, live, eat, pray, enjoy themselves, grieve, and sometimes die, together, they cease to be strangers to each other; no vice remains hidden, no artifice can last. Seen from afar, a caravan looks like a procession; from close to, it is a village, with its stories, jokes, nicknames, intrigues, conflicts, reconciliations, nights of singing and poetry, a village for which all lands are far away, even the land one comes from, or the land one is crossing." p 154 </i></span>
  <a data-text-id="3504112977253886409" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_229677744" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/229677744" />
    <a title="Flávio Sousa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6646190-fl-vio-sousa"><img alt="Flávio Sousa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321222913p2/6646190.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/229677744?book_show_action=true">Nov 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Flávio Sousa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Flávio Sousa" href="/user/show/6646190-fl-vio-sousa">Flávio Sousa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789725648407" href="/book/show/10144691-le-o-o-africano">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6646190-fl-vio-sousa?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer229677744" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14718714577818623846"><i>Leo, Africanus</i> is without a doubt a riveting, epic tale based on the life of a traveler, diplomat, merchant, slave, scholar, etc; whose life would have made a movie, every critic would deride as self-indulgent, irreal and plainly just way over the top. It is in fact an amazing life, who leaves you vicariously humbled as you find more about it.<br/><br/>The book is sectioned in 'Years' which are basicaly chapters spanning roughly a years period (Arab calendar is used). Although this is a nice touch, the fa</span>
  <span id="freeText14718714577818623846" style="display:none"><i>Leo, Africanus</i> is without a doubt a riveting, epic tale based on the life of a traveler, diplomat, merchant, slave, scholar, etc; whose life would have made a movie, every critic would deride as self-indulgent, irreal and plainly just way over the top. It is in fact an amazing life, who leaves you vicariously humbled as you find more about it.<br /><br />The book is sectioned in 'Years' which are basicaly chapters spanning roughly a years period (Arab calendar is used). Although this is a nice touch, the fact of the matter is that in trying to keep the chapters equitative in lenght the book gives off the impression that some chapters are rushed due to the nature of the events going in years of historical significance, while in other chapters/years it feels dragging.<br /><br />The reading also feels, at times, slightly disjointed since the point of view doesn't center it self on Leo until he reaches a certain age where his thoughts and conscience are of a certain maturity and worth sharing. This was of course intentional and a necessary evil (if you can call it that) but i do feel it detracts a bit, eventhough the narrative mantains solid throughout.<br /><br />The book does a fantastic job of describing how Leo might have lived and does paint a layered emotional and psychological portrait of him, but historically it's not until the third part of the book that you feel immersed in the historical period rather than just Leo.<br /><br /><br />Up until the mentioned part of the book, you don't really 'feel' the cultural and historical ambiance (Which is a must for me in Historical fiction works), eventhough there is definitely a reasonably thourough description of how the different cultures lived. <br /><br />For this, and for having read <i>Samarkand</i> before this book, <i>Leo, Africanus</i> pales in comparison by upping the pace a bit too late. Still this book will not fail at managing to immerse, educate and entertain you. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14718714577818623846" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

		    <b>
